srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Map_of_Polish_language.svg/330px-Map_of_Polish_language.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Map_of_Polish_language.svg/440px-Map_of_Polish_language.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2686" data-file-height="2923" /></a><div style="text-align:left;"><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #1E90FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Majority of Polish speakers</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #87CEEB; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Minority of Polish speakers</div></div>
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="noprint selfref" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Replacement_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Polish</b> (<i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">język polski</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">['jɛ̃w̃zɨk ˈpɔlskʲi]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-j%C4%99zyk_polski.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Pl-j%C4%99zyk_polski.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-język polski.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>, <i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">polszczyzna</i>, or simply <i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">polski</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[ˈpɔlskʲi]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-polski.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Pl-polski.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-polski.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>) is a <a href="/wiki/West_Slavic_languages" title="West Slavic languages">West Slavic language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lechitic_languages" title="Lechitic languages">Lechitic</a> group.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It is spoken primarily in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and serves as the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a>. In addition to being an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of Poland, it is also used by <a href="/wiki/Polish_diaspora" title="Polish diaspora">Polish minorities</a> in other countries. There are over 50 million<sup id="cite_ref-encyklopedia_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyklopedia-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-almanac_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-almanac-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Polish-language speakers around the world and it is one of the official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">languages of the European Union</a>. 
</p><p>Polish is written with the standardized <a href="/wiki/Polish_alphabet" title="Polish alphabet">Polish alphabet</a>, which has nine additions to the letters of the basic <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> (<i>ą</i>, <i>ć</i>, <i>ę</i>, <i>ł</i>, <i>ń</i>, <i>ó</i>, <i>ś</i>, <i>ź</i>, <i>ż</i>). Among the major languages, it is most closely related to <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> but differs from other Slavic varieties in terms of pronunciation and general grammar. In addition, Polish was profoundly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, most notably <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic languages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, which contributed to a large number of loanwords and similar grammatical structures.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Polish currently has the largest number of speakers of the West Slavic group and is also the second most widely spoken <a href="/wiki/Slavic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic language">Slavic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cactuslanguagetraining1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cactuslanguagetraining1-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ethnologue1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, Polish was a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> important both diplomatically and academically in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. Today, Polish is spoken by over 38.5 million people as their first language in Poland. It is also spoken as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, western parts of <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> as well as in central-western <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>. Because of the emigration from Poland during different time periods, most notably after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, millions of Polish speakers can be found in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, (the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Polish began to emerge as a distinct language around the 10th century, the process largely triggered by the establishment and development of the Polish state. <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko I</a>, ruler of the Polans tribe from the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland" title="Greater Poland">Greater Poland</a> region, united a few culturally and linguistically related tribes from the basins of the <a href="/wiki/Vistula" title="Vistula">Vistula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oder" title="Oder">Oder</a> before eventually accepting baptism in 966. With Christianity, Poland also adopted the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, which made it possible to write down Polish, which until then had existed only as a <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG/220px-Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="55" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG/330px-Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG/440px-Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w" title="Book of Henryków">Book of Henryków</a> is the earliest document to include a sentence written entirely in what can be interpreted as <a href="/wiki/Old_Polish_language" title="Old Polish language">Old Polish</a> - <i>Day, ut ia pobrusa, a ti poziwai</i> highlighted in red</div></div></div>
<p>The precursor to modern Polish is the <a href="/wiki/Old_Polish_language" title="Old Polish language">Old Polish language</a>. Ultimately, Polish is thought to descend from the unattested <a href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a> language. Polish was a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> from 1500–1700 in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and small portions of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, because of the political, cultural, scientific and military influence of the former <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Although not closely related to it, Polish shares many linguistic  affinities with <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, an <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">East Slavic language</a> with which it has been in prolonged historical contact and in a state of mutual influence.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The Polish influence on Ukrainian is particularly marked in western Ukraine, which was under Polish cultural domination.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Henryk%C3%B3w" title="Book of Henryków">Book of Henryków</a> (Polish: <i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">Księga henrykowska</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Liber fundationis claustri Sanctae Mariae Virginis in Heinrichau</i>), contains the earliest known sentence written in the Polish language: <i>Day, ut ia pobrusa, a ti poziwai</i> (in modern orthography: <i>Daj, uć ja pobrusza, a ti pocziwaj,</i> the corresponding sentence in modern Polish: <i>Daj, niech ja pomielę, a ty odpoczywaj</i> or <i>Pozwól, że ja będę mełł, a ty odpocznij</i>, English: <i>Come, let me grind, and you take a rest</i>), written around 1270.
</p><p>The medieval recorder of this phrase, the Cistercian monk Peter of the Henryków monastery, noted that "Hoc est in polonico" ("This is in Polish").<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Polish, along with Czech and Slovak, forms the West Slavic <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>. The three languages constitute <a href="/wiki/Ausbau_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ausbau languages">Ausbau languages</a>, i.e. lects that are considered distinct not on purely  linguistic grounds, but rather due to sociopolitical and cultural factors.<sup id="cite_ref-A_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Since the idioms have separately <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standardized norms</a> and longstanding literary traditions, being the official languages of independent states, they are generally treated as autonomous languages, with the distinction between Polish and Czech-Slovak dialects being drawn along national lines.<sup id="cite_ref-A_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_Polish_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of Polish speakers">Geographical distribution of Polish speakers</a></div>
<p>Poland is the most linguistically <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Homogeneous" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:Homogeneous">homogeneous</a> European country; nearly 97% of Poland's citizens declare Polish as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>. Elsewhere, <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a> constitute large minorities in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. Polish is the most widely used minority language in Lithuania's <a href="/wiki/Vilnius_County" title="Vilnius County">Vilnius County</a> (26% of the population, according to the 2001 census results, with <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a> having been part of Poland from 1922 until 1939) and is found elsewhere in southeastern Lithuania. In Ukraine, it is most common in western <a href="/wiki/Lviv_Oblast" title="Lviv Oblast">Lviv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volyn_Oblast" title="Volyn Oblast">Volyn Oblasts</a>, while in <a href="/wiki/West_Belarus" class="mw-redirect" title="West Belarus">West Belarus</a> it is used by the significant Polish minority, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Brest,_Belarus" title="Brest, Belarus">Brest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grodno" title="Grodno">Grodno</a> regions and in areas along the Lithuanian border. There are significant numbers of Polish speakers among <a href="/wiki/Polish_diaspora" title="Polish diaspora">Polish emigrants</a> and their descendants in many other countries.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_Americans" title="Polish Americans">Polish Americans</a> number more than 11 million but most of them cannot speak Polish fluently. According to the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">2000 United States Census</a>, 667,414  Americans of age five years and over reported Polish as the language spoken at home, which is about 1.4% of people who speak languages other than <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, 0.25% of the US population, and 6% of the Polish-American population. The largest concentrations of Polish speakers reported in the census (over 50%) were found in three states: <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> (185,749), <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> (111,740), and <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> (74,663).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Enough people in these areas speak Polish that <a href="/wiki/PNC_Financial_Services" title="PNC Financial Services">PNC Financial Services</a> (which has a large number of branches in all of these areas) offer services available in Polish at all of their <a href="/wiki/Cash_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash machine">cash machines</a> in addition to <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2011 census there are now over 500,000 people in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> who consider Polish to be their "main" language. In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, there is a significant <a href="/wiki/Polish_Canadians" title="Polish Canadians">Polish Canadian population</a>: There are 242,885 speakers of Polish according to the 2006 census, with a particular concentration in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> (91,810 speakers) and <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-census_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The geographical distribution of the Polish language was greatly affected by the <a href="/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_Poland_immediately_after_World_War_II" title="Territorial changes of Poland immediately after World War II">territorial changes of Poland immediately after World War II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polish_population_transfers_(1944%E2%80%9346)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish population transfers (1944–46)">Polish population transfers (1944–46)</a>. Poles settled in the "<a href="/wiki/Recovered_Territories" title="Recovered Territories">Recovered Territories</a>" in the west and north, which had previously been mostly <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>-speaking. Some Poles remained in the previously Polish-ruled territories in the east that were annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a>, resulting in the present-day Polish-speaking minorities in Lithuania, Belarus, and Ukraine, although many Poles were expelled or emigrated from those areas to areas within Poland's new borders. To the east of Poland, the most significant Polish minority lives in a long, narrow strip along either side of the <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>-<a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> border. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_(1944%E2%80%9350)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flight and expulsion of Germans (1944–50)">flight and expulsion of Germans (1944–50)</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Poland_and_Soviet_Ukraine" title="Population exchange between Poland and Soviet Ukraine">expulsion of Ukrainians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Vistula" title="Operation Vistula">Operation Vistula</a>, the 1947 forced resettlement of Ukrainian minorities to the Recovered Territories in the west of the country, contributed to the country's linguistic homogeneity.
</p>

href="/wiki/Recovered_Territories" title="Recovered Territories">Recovered Territories</a>" (in pink) were parts of Germany, including the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Free City of Danzig</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>), that became part of Poland after World War II. Gray color, territories lost to the Soviet Union followed by mass <a href="/wiki/Polish_population_transfers_(1944%E2%80%9346)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish population transfers (1944–46)">Polish population transfers (1944–46)</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:277px;max-width:277px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG/275px-Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG" decoding="async" width="275" height="315" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG/413px-Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG/550px-Languages_of_CE_Europe-3.PNG 2x" data-file-width="951" data-file-height="1088" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Geographical distribution of the Polish language (green) and other Central and Eastern European languages and dialects. A large Polish-speaking diaspora remains in the countries located east of Poland that were once the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Borderlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Borderlands">Eastern Borderlands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a> (1918-1939).</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:318px;max-width:318px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png/316px-J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png" decoding="async" width="316" height="315" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png/474px-J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png/632px-J%C4%99zyk_polski_w_Europie.png 2x" data-file-width="1002" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Knowledge of the Polish language within <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. The Polish diaspora heavily contributed to the spread of the Polish language in Western European countries (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>. However, it was the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> that made Polish <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> in Central and Eastern Europe as well as in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic States">Baltic States</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dialects_of_Polish" title="Dialects of Polish">Dialects of Polish</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG/220px-Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="378" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG/330px-Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG/440px-Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG 2x" data-file-width="463" data-file-height="796" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Modlitwy_drukowane_po_polsku_w_r._1475.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The oldest printed text in Polish &#8211; <i>Statuta synodalia Episcoporum Wratislaviensis</i> printed in 1475 in <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a> by Kasper Elyan.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish-alphabet.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Polish-alphabet.png/220px-Polish-alphabet.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Polish-alphabet.png/330px-Polish-alphabet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Polish-alphabet.png/440px-Polish-alphabet.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish-alphabet.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Polish alphabet contains 32 letters. Q, V and X are not used in the Polish language.</div></div></div>
<p>The Polish language became far more homogeneous in the second half of the 20th century, in part due to the mass migration of several million Polish citizens from the eastern to the western part of the country after the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> annexation of the <a href="/wiki/Kresy" title="Kresy">Kresy</a> (Eastern Borderlands) in 1939, and the annexation of <a href="/wiki/Former_eastern_territories_of_Germany" title="Former eastern territories of Germany">former German territory</a> after World War II. This tendency toward homogeneity also stems from the vertically integrated nature of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People&#39;s Republic">Polish People's Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Polish linguistics has been characterized by a strong strive towards promoting <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_prescription" title="Linguistic prescription">prescriptive</a> ideas of language intervention and usage uniformity,<sup id="cite_ref-mling_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mling-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> along with normatively-oriented notions of language "correctness",<sup id="cite_ref-wtad_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wtad-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> unusually by Western standards.<sup id="cite_ref-mling_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mling-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The inhabitants of different regions of Poland still<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> speak Polish somewhat differently, although the differences between modern-day <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> varieties and standard Polish (<i lang="pl" title="Polish language text">język ogólnopolski</i>) appear relatively slight. Most of the middle aged and young speak vernaculars close to standard Polish, while the traditional dialects are preserved among older people in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-wtad_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wtad-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> First-language speakers of Polish have no trouble understanding each other, and non-native speakers may have difficulty recognizing the regional and <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">social</a> differences. The modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard dialect</a>, often termed as "correct Polish",<sup id="cite_ref-wtad_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wtad-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> is spoken or at least understood throughout the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Polish has traditionally been described as consisting of four or five main dialects:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland" title="Greater Poland">Greater Polish</a>, spoken in the west</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lesser_Poland" title="Lesser Poland">Lesser Polish</a>, spoken in the south and southeast</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Masovian_dialect" title="Masovian dialect">Masovian</a>, spoken throughout the central and eastern parts of the country</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Silesian_language" title="Silesian language">Silesian</a>, spoken in the southwest (also considered a separate language, see comment below)</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kashubian_language" title="Kashubian language">Kashubian</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a> west of <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, is thought of either as a fifth <a href="/wiki/Dialects_of_Polish" title="Dialects of Polish">Polish dialect</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Ausbau" class="mw-redirect" title="Ausbau">distinct language</a>, depending on the criteria used.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>. It contains a number of features not found elsewhere in Poland, e.g. nine distinct oral vowels (vs. the five of standard Polish) and (in the northern dialects) phonemic word stress, an archaic feature preserved from <a href="/wiki/Common_Slavic" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Slavic">Common Slavic</a> times and not found anywhere else among the <a href="/wiki/West_Slavic_languages" title="West Slavic languages">West Slavic languages</a>. However, it "lacks most of the linguistic and social determinants of language-hood".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many linguistic sources about the Slavic languages describe Silesian as a dialect of Polish.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> However, many Silesians consider themselves a separate ethnicity and have been advocating for the recognition of a Silesian language. According to the last official census in Poland in 2011, over half a million people declared Silesian as their native language. Many sociolinguists (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tomasz_Kamusella" title="Tomasz Kamusella">Tomasz Kamusella</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Agnieszka Pianka, Alfred F. Majewicz,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tomasz_Wicherkiewicz" title="Tomasz Wicherkiewicz">Tomasz Wicherkiewicz</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> assume that extralinguistic criteria decide whether a lect is an independent language or a dialect: speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">speech variety</a> or/and political decisions, and this is dynamic (i.e. it changes over time). Also, research organizations such as <a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">SIL International</a><sup id="cite_ref-SIL_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIL-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and resources for the academic field of linguistics such as <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Linguist_List" title="Linguist List">Linguist List</a><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and others, for example the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Administration_and_Digitization_(Poland)" title="Ministry of Administration and Digitization (Poland)">Ministry of Administration and Digitization</a><sup id="cite_ref-MoAaD_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoAaD-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> recognized the Silesian language. In July 2007, the Silesian language was recognized by <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>, and was attributed an ISO code of szl.
</p><p>Some additional characteristic but less widespread regional dialects include:
</p>
<ol><li>The distinctive dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Gorals#language" title="Gorals">Gorals</a> (<i>Góralski</i>) occurs in the mountainous area bordering the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>. The Gorals ("Highlanders") take great pride in their culture and the dialect. It exhibits some cultural influences from the <a href="/wiki/Vlachs" title="Vlachs">Vlach</a> shepherds who migrated from <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> (southern Romania) in the 14th–17th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gwara_pozna%C5%84ska" class="extiw" title="pl:Gwara poznańska">Poznanski dialect</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a> and to some extent in the whole region of the former <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Partition" title="Prussian Partition">Prussian Partition</a> (excluding <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia" title="Upper Silesia">Upper Silesia</a>), with noticeable German influences.</li>
<li>In the northern and western (formerly German) regions where Poles from the territories annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> resettled after World War II, the older generation speaks a dialect of Polish characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/Kresy" title="Kresy">Kresy</a> that includes a longer pronunciation of vowels.</li>
<li>Poles living in Lithuania (particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a> region), in Belarus (particularly the northwest), and in the northeast of Poland continue to speak the <a href="/wiki/Kresy" title="Kresy">Eastern Borderlands</a> dialect, which sounds "slushed" (in Polish described as <i>zaciąganie z ruska</i>, "speaking with a Ruthenian drawl") and is easily distinguishable.</li>
<li>Some city dwellers, especially the less affluent population, had their own distinctive dialects - for example, the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_dialect" title="Warsaw dialect">Warsaw dialect</a>, still spoken by some of the population of <a href="/wiki/Praga" title="Praga">Praga</a> on the eastern bank of the <a href="/wiki/Vistula" title="Vistula">Vistula</a>. (Praga remained the only part of Warsaw where the population survived World War II relatively intact.) However, these city dialects are now<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> mostly extinct due to assimilation with standard Polish.</li>
<li>Many Poles living in emigrant communities (for example, in the USA), whose families left Poland just after World War II, retain a number of minor features of Polish vocabulary as spoken in the first half of the 20th century that now sound archaic to contemporary visitors from Poland.</li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polish_phonology" title="Polish phonology">Polish phonology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="68.185396825397" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Pl-Deflegmator-article.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Pl-Deflegmator-article.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (77 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="77094" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/62/Pl-Deflegmator-article.ogg/Pl-Deflegmator-article.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="155792" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Deflegmator-article.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spoken Polish in a neutral informative tone</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels_and_consonants">Vowels and consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vowels and consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Polish has six oral vowels (all <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthongs</a>) and two <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal vowels</a>. The oral vowels are <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a>/</span> (spelled <i>i</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid central unrounded vowel">ɨ</a>/</span> (spelled <i>y</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ</a>/</span> (spelled <i>e</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>/</span> (spelled <i>a</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a>/</span> (spelled <i>o</i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a>/</span> (spelled <i>u</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%93" title="Ó">ó</a></i>). The nasal vowels are <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">ɛ̃</a>/</span> (spelled <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%98" title="Ę">ę</a></i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">ɔ̃</a>/</span> (spelled <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%84" title="Ą">ą</a></i>).
</p><p>The Polish <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> system shows more complexity: its characteristic features include the series of <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">affricate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palatal_consonant" title="Palatal consonant">palatal consonants</a> that resulted from four <a href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a> <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalizations</a> and two further palatalizations that took place in Polish and <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a>. The full set of consonants, together with their most common spellings, can be presented as follows (although other phonological analyses exist):
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">stops</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_stop" title="Voiceless bilabial stop">p</a>/</span> (<i>p</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_stop" title="Voiced bilabial stop">b</a>/</span> (<i>b</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_stop" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless dental stop">t</a>/</span> (<i>t</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_stop" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced dental stop">d</a>/</span> (<i>d</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_stop" title="Voiceless velar stop">k</a>/</span> (<i>k</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>/</span> (<i>g</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalized</a> forms <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">kʲ</a>/</span> (<i>ki</i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">ɡʲ</a>/</span> (<i>gi</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricatives</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiceless labiodental fricative">f</a>/</span> (<i>f</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">v</a>/</span> (<i>w</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_sibilant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless dental sibilant">s</a>/</span> (<i>s</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_sibilant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced dental sibilant">z</a>/</span> (<i>z</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiceless retroflex fricative">ʂ</a>/</span> (<i>sz</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_fricative" title="Voiced retroflex fricative">ʐ</a>/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/%C5%BB" title="Ż">ż</a>, rz</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_consonant" title="Alveolo-palatal consonant">alveolo-palatals</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal fricative">ɕ</a>/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A" title="Ś">ś</a>, si</i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal fricative">ʑ</a>/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/%C5%B9" title="Ź">ź</a>, zi</i>), and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a>/</span> (<i>ch, h</i>) and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">xʲ</a>/</span> (<i>chi, hi</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">affricates</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless dental affricate">ts</a>/</span> (<i>c</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced dental affricate">dz</a>/</span> (<i>dz</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_retroflex_affricate" title="Voiceless retroflex affricate">tʂ</a>/</span> (<i>cz</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_affricate" title="Voiced retroflex affricate">dʐ</a>/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/D%C5%BC" class="mw-redirect" title="Dż">dż</a>, drz</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal affricate">tɕ</a>/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/%C4%86" title="Ć">ć</a>, ci</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal affricate">dʑ</a>/</span> (<i>dź, dzi</i>) (these are written here without <a href="/wiki/Tie_(typography)" title="Tie (typography)">ties</a> for browser display compatibility, although Polish does distinguish between affricates as in <i>czy</i>, and stop&#8211;fricative clusters as in <i>trzy</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">nasals</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Bilabial_nasal" title="Bilabial nasal">m</a>/</span> (<i>m</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Dental_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental nasal">n</a>/</span> (<i>n</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolo-palatal nasal">ɲ</a>/</span> (<i>ń, ni</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">approximants</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">l</a>/</span> (<i>l</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant" title="Palatal approximant">j</a>/</span> (<i>j</i>), <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93velar_approximant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced labial–velar approximant">w</a>/</span> (<i>ł</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trill_consonant" title="Trill consonant">trill</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar trill">r</a>/</span> (<i>r</i>)</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish_vowel_chart_(with_allophones).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg/220px-Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg/330px-Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg/440px-Polish_vowel_chart_%28with_allophones%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="754" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish_vowel_chart_(with_allophones).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polish oral vowels depicted on a vowel chart. Main <a href="/wiki/Allophones" class="mw-redirect" title="Allophones">allophones</a> (in black) are in broad transcription, whereas positional allophones (in red and green) are in narrow transcription. Allophones with red dots appear in palatal contexts. The central vowel <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐ</a>&#93;</span> is an unstressed allophone of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛ, ɔ, a/</span> in certain contexts</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Neutralization_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutralization (linguistics)">Neutralization</a> occurs between <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a>–<a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a> consonant pairs in certain environments: at the end of words (where devoicing occurs), and in certain consonant clusters (where <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(phonology)" title="Assimilation (phonology)">assimilation</a> occurs). For details, see <i><a href="/wiki/Polish_phonology#Voicing_and_devoicing" title="Polish phonology">Voicing and devoicing</a></i> in the article on Polish phonology.
</p><p>Most Polish words are <a href="/wiki/Paroxytone" title="Paroxytone">paroxytones</a> (that is, the <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a> falls on the second-to-last syllable of a polysyllabic word), although there are exceptions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_distribution">Consonant distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Consonant distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Polish permits complex consonant clusters, which historically often arose from the disappearance of <a href="/wiki/Yer" title="Yer">yers</a>. Polish can have word-initial and word-medial clusters of up to four consonants, whereas word-final clusters can have up to five consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Examples of such clusters can be found in words such as <i>bezwzględny</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[bɛzˈvzɡlɛndnɨ]</span> ('absolute' or 'heartless', 'ruthless'), <i>źdźbło</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈʑd͡ʑbwɔ]</span> ('blade of grass'), <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-wstrz%C4%85s.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Pl-wstrz%C4%85s.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-wstrząs.ogg"><i>wstrząs</i></a></span></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈfstʂɔw̃s]</span> ('shock'), and <i>krnąbrność</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈkrnɔmbrnɔɕt͡ɕ]</span> ('disobedience'). A popular Polish <a href="/wiki/Tongue-twister" title="Tongue-twister">tongue-twister</a> (from a verse by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Brzechwa" title="Jan Brzechwa">Jan Brzechwa</a>) is <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polish_Tongue_twister_-_Chrz%C4%85szcz_-_2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/Polish_Tongue_twister_-_Chrz%C4%85szcz_-_2.ogg" class="internal" title="Polish Tongue twister - Chrząszcz - 2.ogg"><i>W Szczebrzeszynie chrząszcz brzmi w trzcinie</i></a></span></span> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[fʂt͡ʂɛbʐɛˈʂɨɲɛ ˈxʂɔw̃ʂt͡ʂ ˈbʐmi fˈtʂt͡ɕiɲɛ]</span> ('In <a href="/wiki/Szczebrzeszyn" title="Szczebrzeszyn">Szczebrzeszyn</a> a beetle buzzes in the reed').
</p><p>Unlike languages such as <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>, Polish does not have <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_consonant" title="Syllabic consonant">syllabic consonants</a> – the nucleus of a <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a> is always a vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The consonant <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span> is restricted to positions adjacent to a vowel. It also cannot precede <i>i</i> or <i>y</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosody">Prosody</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prosody">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The predominant <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a> pattern in Polish is penultimate stress – in a word of more than one syllable, the next-to-last syllable is stressed. Alternating preceding syllables carry secondary stress, e.g. in a four-syllable word, where the primary stress is on the third syllable, there will be secondary stress on the first.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each vowel represents one syllable, although the letter <i>i</i> normally does not represent a vowel when it precedes another vowel (it represents <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/j/</span>, palatalization of the preceding consonant, or both depending on analysis). Also the letters <i>u</i> and <i>i</i> sometimes represent only semivowels when they follow another vowel, as in <i>autor</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ˈawtɔr/</span> ('author'), mostly in loanwords (so not in native <i>nauka</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/naˈu.ka/</span> 'science, the act of learning', for example, nor in nativized <i>Mateusz</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/maˈte.uʂ/</span>  'Matthew').
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg/220px-Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg/330px-Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg/440px-Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3488" data-file-height="2616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Styl_urzedowy_-_Polish_sign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A formal-tone informative sign in Polish, with a composition of vowels and consonants and a mixture of long, medium and short <a href="/wiki/Syllables" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllables">syllables</a></div></div></div>  
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>, particularly from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical languages</a>, have the stress on the antepenultimate (third-from-last) syllable. For example, <i>fizyka</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ˈfizɨka/</span>) ('physics') is stressed on the first syllable. This may lead to a rare phenomenon of minimal pairs differing only in stress placement, for example <i>muzyka</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ˈmuzɨka/</span> 'music' vs. <i>muzyka</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/muˈzɨka/</span> - genitive singular of <i>muzyk</i> 'musician'. When additional syllables are added to such words through <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a> or <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixation</a>, the stress normally becomes regular. For example, <i>uniwersytet</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uɲiˈvɛrsɨtɛt/</span>, 'university') has irregular stress on the third (or antepenultimate) syllable, but the genitive <i>uniwersytetu</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uɲivɛrsɨˈtɛtu/</span>) and derived adjective <i>uniwersytecki</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/uɲivɛrsɨˈtɛt͡skʲi/</span>) have regular stress on the penultimate syllables. Over time, loanwords become nativized to have penultimate stress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGussmann20079_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGussmann20079-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another class of exceptions is verbs with the conditional endings <i>-by, -bym, -byśmy</i>, etc. These endings are not counted in determining the position of the stress; for example, <i><b>zro</b>biłbym</i> ('I would do') is stressed on the first syllable, and <i>zro<b>bi</b>libyśmy</i> ('we would do') on the second. According to <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_prescription" title="Linguistic prescription">prescriptive authorities</a>, the same applies to the first and second person plural past tense endings <i>-śmy, -ście</i>, although this rule is often ignored in <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">colloquial speech</a> (so <i>zro<b>bi</b>liśmy</i> 'we did' should be prescriptively stressed on the second syllable, although in <a href="/wiki/Usus" title="Usus">practice</a> it is commonly stressed on the third as <i>zrobi<b>li</b>śmy</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> These irregular stress patterns are explained by the fact that these endings are detachable <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitics</a> rather than true verbal inflections: for example, instead of <i><b>ko</b>go zoba<b>czy</b>liście?</i> ('whom did you see?') it is possible to say <i><b>ko</b>goście zoba<b>czy</b>li?</i> – here <i>kogo</i> retains its usual stress (first syllable) in spite of the attachment of the clitic. Reanalysis of the endings as inflections when attached to verbs causes the different colloquial stress patterns. These stress patterns are however nowadays sanctioned as part of the colloquial norm of standard Polish.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some common word combinations are stressed as if they were a single word. This applies in particular to many combinations of preposition plus a personal pronoun, such as <i><b>do</b> niej</i> ('to her'), <i><b>na</b> nas</i> ('on us'), <i>prze<b>ze</b> mnie</i> ('because of me'), all stressed on the bolded syllable.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Polish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Polish_orthography" title="Polish orthography">Polish orthography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polish_Braille" title="Polish Braille">Polish Braille</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Polish_alphabet" title="Polish alphabet">Polish alphabet</a> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, but includes certain additional letters formed using <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritics</a>. The Polish alphabet was one of three major forms of Latin-based orthography developed for Slavic languages, the others being <a href="/wiki/Czech_orthography" title="Czech orthography">Czech orthography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian orthography</a>, the last of these being a 19th-century invention trying to make a compromise between the first two. <a href="/wiki/Kashubian_language" title="Kashubian language">Kashubian</a> uses a Polish-based system, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a> uses a Czech-based system, and <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a> follows the Croatian one; the <a href="/wiki/Sorbian_languages" title="Sorbian languages">Sorbian languages</a> blend the Polish and the Czech ones.
</p><p>The diacritics used in the Polish alphabet are the <i>kreska</i> (graphically similar to the <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a>) in the letters <i>ć, ń, ó, ś, ź</i> and through the letter in <i>ł</i>; the <i>kropka</i> (superior dot) in the letter <i>ż</i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Ogonek" title="Ogonek">ogonek</a></i> ("little tail") in the letters <i>ą, ę</i>. The letters <i>q, v, x</i> are often not considered part of the Polish alphabet; they are used only in foreign words and names.
</p><p>Polish orthography is largely <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemic</a>—there is a consistent correspondence between letters (or <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">digraphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trigraph_(orthography)" title="Trigraph (orthography)">trigraphs</a>) and phonemes (for exceptions see below). The letters of the alphabet and their normal phonemic values are listed in the following table.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg/220px-Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg/330px-Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg/440px-Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="909" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wyjscia_wujek_1599.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Jakub_Wujek_Bible" title="Jakub Wujek Bible">Jakub Wujek Bible</a> in Polish, 1599 print. The letters á and é were subsequently abolished, but survive in Czech.</div></div></div>

<td><b>zi</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal fricative">ʑ</a>/</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Voiced consonant letters frequently come to represent voiceless sounds (as shown in the tables); this occurs at the end of words and in certain clusters, due to the neutralization mentioned in the <i><a href="#Phonology">Phonology</a></i> section above. Occasionally also voiceless consonant letters can represent voiced sounds in clusters.
</p><p>The spelling rule for the palatal sounds <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal fricative">ɕ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal fricative">ʑ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal affricate">tɕ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiced alveolo-palatal affricate">dʑ</a>/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_nasal" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolo-palatal nasal">ɲ</a>/</span> is as follows: before the vowel <i>i</i> the plain letters <i>s, z, c, dz, n</i> are used; before other vowels the combinations <i>si, zi, ci, dzi, ni</i> are used; when not followed by a vowel the diacritic forms <i>ś, ź, ć, dź, ń</i> are used. For example, the <i>s</i> in <i>siwy</i> ("grey-haired"), the <i>si</i> in <i>siarka</i> ("sulphur") and the <i>ś</i> in  <i>święty</i> ("holy") all represent the sound <span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal fricative">ɕ</a>/</span>. The exceptions to the above rule are certain loanwords from Latin, Italian, French, Russian or English—where <i>s</i> before <i>i</i> is pronounced as <i>s</i>, e.g. <i>sinus</i>, <i>sinologia</i>, <i>do re mi fa sol la si do</i>, <i>Saint-Simon i saint-simoniści</i>, <i>Sierioża</i>, <i>Siergiej</i>, <i>Singapur</i>, <i>singiel</i>. In other loanwords the vowel <i>i</i> is changed to <i>y</i>, e.g. <i>Syria</i>, <i>Sybir</i>, <i>synchronizacja</i>, <i>Syrakuzy</i>.
</p><p>The following table shows the correspondence between the sounds and spelling:
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">Digraphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trigraph_(orthography)" title="Trigraph (orthography)">trigraphs</a> are used:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<th>Phonemic value
</th>

</th>
<th>Digraph/Trigraph<br />(before a vowel)
</th>
<th>Single letter/Digraph<br />(before the vowel <i>i</i>)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolo-palatal_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolo-palatal affricate">tɕ</a>/</span>
</td>
